(a) Is he aware that—

asked the Minister of Public Administration and HomeAffairs:

(a) Is he aware that the Government pledged to createhundreds of thousands jobs in the Public Serviceas stated in the Presidential Manifesto in 2005?

(b) Will he inform this House whether the above pledgehas been fulfilled by the Government?

(c) Will he admit that,-

(i) the new young voters who expected jobs havebeen frustrated and lost their hopes;

(ii) it is a very dangerous trend keeping in mind theinsurrection of 1971 and 1988?

(d) Will he mention the sectors that the Governmenttargeted to provide jobs in?

(e) Will he inform this House sparately the number ofpeople retired from service as per year basis for thelast 05 years?

(f) If not, why?

asked the Minister of Road Development and DeputyMinister of Public Administration:

(a) Will he state in this House,—

(i) separately, the amount of money allocated foreach road in the Nagoda Divisional SecretariatDivision in the Galle District under the “MagaNeguma” Project in 2005;

(ii) when the said roads are considered separately,the nature of the respective projects such aswhether it is a laying of concrete, a coating oftar or a road repair;

ªÇæ Ç ¨Çæ¸å¾åÆ¨ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ ¼ ¯ÝµÇ·Ç»¯Æ¯)(The Hon. Ravi Karunanayake)Thank you, Mr. Deputy Chairman of Committees, for givingme this opportunity to speak on a very important day onwhich almost six Bills have been brought in on two, threedifferent topics.

In the morning you would have heard the commotion whenthis august Assembly started. This is the present state of affarsin the Government. The Government Members do not knowexactly what is happening in Parliament. The Leader of theHouse mentioned that Item Eight would not be brought inand the Hon. Minister of Disaster Management and HumanRights gets up and says, “No it will betaken up.” We wantedto make a statement and it was debated as to whether thatcould be done or not. Now this is the exact replication ofwhat is taking place in the rest of the country. The macro -economic policy of this country is put in to serious jeopardy;it is being tested beyond its limit.

Today, Sir, the world is looking at Sri Lanka and wonderingwhat is going on. People are looking at the peace processwith a lot of circumspection. The investors are looking at theGovernment, which is constituted of a heterogeneous group,to see whether it is getting its act together. Because we havea dominant People’s Alliance with a chauvinistic, anarchicJVP, and a Hela Urumaya of support it. Now they are lookingat the direction that this Government is heading. Likewise,you see external dimensions into the internal working of thiscountry. The co-chairs, which came into existence becauseof the foresightedness of the Ceasefire Agreement, now seento be going forward. Now you can see that the Indans, withwhom we have extremely good neighbourly relations, arelooking at our country. They They are also looking at theirgeo-political situation. You have a Government today that isforgetting its closest neighbour and trying to do economicswith neighbours that are far away in our region, China on oneside, Pakistan on the other and as a result, our neighbour,who comes to our assistance at any given moment iswondering what is going on.

Then, Sir, also the people are getting frustrated. ThisGovernment was electd almost two years and four monthsago. The Executive President has been government and rulingthis country in a way that he deems fit for the last nine months.The people were expecting major changes to take place.

Likewise, the coalition partners are disjointed. They arelooking at what is in it for them. That is, each party in theGovernment is looking at personal gain. They are runningwith the hare and hunting with the hounds.

Amidst this, Sir today many financial amendments havebeen brought in, to suit certain things. Certainly, they arematters, may be too little too late, but we appreciate that theyhave been looked into. We have taken up the Recovery ofLoans by Banks (Special Provisions) (Amendment) Bill. Thenwe are also debating the National Insurance Trust Fund Billthe Banking (Amendment) Bill, the Payment Devices FraudsBill, the Non-Performing Assets (Recovery) Bill, the Gaming(Special Provisions) Bill, the Resolution under theAppropriation Act, and the Select Committee of Parliamenton the Seventeenth Amendment to the Constitution.

If I may take them one by one, Sir, first I would like to take upthe Recovery of Loans by Banks (Special Provisions)(Amendment) Bill. Look at the intention of the Governmentfor bringing this Bill. At this particular moment, theGovernment, owing to certain mismanagement that is goingon, is trying to do certain things to resuscitate, give a newlook to it. In the process, they are looking at what can bedone. The first thing that we are looking at in this amendmentBill which we mentioned at the Consultative Committee onFinance and Planning is that the fact that any person whoowes less than Rs. 5 million to the bank should be exemptedfrom Parate execution.

Sir, today the price of even two perches in Slave Island isabout Rs. 8 to 9 million, So, what does Rs. 5 million amountto ? If you are looking at protecting the small and the medium- scale enterpreneurs the down trodden, the person whosevery existence depends on the bank’s funding. What is Rs. 5million? We, from the United National Party, have alwaysbeen considerate and we are proposing that this be increasedto nothing less than Rs. 50 million. Ensure that people whoowe less than Rs. 50 million do not come within thisdraconian law of parate execution. I am looking at theessence of it. The inflationary trends that have taken placehave distorted the actual position. So that Rs. 5 million meansnothing. Therefore, we request the Government to increasethis to Rs. 50 million.

Then Sir, the Hon. Deputy Minister was posed with thequestion from the Opposition - the Hon. Anura Dissanayakeasked him - whether the people who have taken money fromthese public banks can be named. Obviously the DeputyMinister, due to the lack of his knowledge on legal matters,said, “Yes, you can reveal who the debtors are to this augustAssembly.”

That is not the case. Actually, there is an element of secrecy.So, the only way that you can do it is by going to court andgetting an order to make it public. If you go to court, thenyou can certainly do it. But, we, from the United NationalParty, want you to bring in regulations. We tell theGovernment, “Please, bring in regulations, to name in thisaugust Assembly, any person who owes a public bank morethan Rs. 100 million, and is a delinquent and is purposelyevading payment.

Quit rightly, the small borrower, is obviously a verygenuine payer. The History of public banks would revealthat payments have been no problem from the small andmedium - scale borrowers. It is the big sharks that utilize thelacunae in the laws and make use of public funds to fulfiltheir ulterior motives. Sir. Therefore, we would greatlyappreciate if an amendment or a special Bill could be broughtin throug which any person who has taken more than Rs.100 million and whose loan has been non-performing for acertain period, may be beyond six months or nine months,can be named in this august Assembly so that you do notneed to resort to legal procedures to do that . We are asking

for this to protect the decent, the innocent, borrower and thatis certainly something which we want to bring up at thisparticular moment.

Then, Sir, if you look at the Banking (Amendment) Bill,this is something that is too little, too late. You have theCentral Bank, performing a supervisory role in this country.If by chance, the Central Bank’s supervisory division has notperformed up to expectation, then there could be great errorsin its supervisory functions. Bringing in separate Bills inorder to correct the inefficiency there, is not the answer. Thishas been mentioned at the Consultative Committee and weasked, “Is this not too little, too late? Why is the supervisoryrole not Functioning ?” Give due credit to the Central Bank.There may be other banks which have crashed in the recentpast. This may be just one such bank. The Central Bank mayhave protected one or two such banks. So, on that basis, thereis a definite indication that they have been doing their job ina proper way. In that case, what is all this about? What are theState Banks that are having this problem that needs to berectified, to be corrected? The Hon. Deputy Minister ofFinance and Planning is not here, the Hon. Minister of Financeand Planning is not here. So, with whom are we debatingthese in this House, Hon. Deputy Chairman ? last collected was around Rs. 284 million which wasdrastically reduced to Rs. 164 million in the year 2005. I donot know what it is in 2006. But we are saying this withexperience. The war that we should have taken to theLTTE’s doorstep, the LTTE has brought it on to our doorstep.This is the irony of the mismanagement of this Government.We are asking “It is the same rhetoric that they are doing onthe economic front?” They laughed at the United NationalParty. They laughed at the experience and the ability of theUnited National Party. Today, they are in an absolute mess.The Indian Government is asking, “Can you not get your acttogether?” That is the irony. I am talking of the front-pagepicture of the Sunday Times of July 9th. That is regarding theinnocent children who have not even seen a metal detector.When they go to school they have to learn what metaldetectors are. That is the level that this country has descendedto.

Then about Lieutenant General Parami Kulatunga whodied recently. His sister is a saying that the brother is no moreamong the living owing to the fact that the army and the

But, that does not mean that othercountries have a right to come and stifle us and basicallymanipulate certain political parties. India with whom we havean extremely close relationsship will ensure that they willtake on anybody who tries to destabilize us. So, on that basisthe Government must realize the geopolitical situation inthis country. (Dr) Sarath Amunugama - Minister of PublicAdministration and Home Affairs)Mr. Deputy Chairman of Committees, before I go intodetails of the Bills that we are discussing in Parliamenttoday, I would like to respond to some of the criticisms madeby my good Friend, the Hon. Ravi Karunanayake.

His speech was a diatribe, a collection of all the critritismsthat are made by the UNP on public platforms. I think thatthese criticisms in the UNP are both internal and external.Going by the news papers he has made similar criticismsinternally as well. But that is a matter for him. I only wnatto tell you that the objective situation is quite differentfrom what he says.

I refer you, Sir, to the Annual Report of the Central Bankfor 2005 which I think is a more reliable analysis of theeconomy of this country. Let me deal with the commentson the development of financial institutions. That was amatter my Hon. Friend referred to. The actualmacroeconomic situation is as follows, Sir.

I quote, page 126, Chapter 8.2 of the Annual Report ofthe Central Bank for 2005, under the heading,“Developments in Financial Institutions,” it states :

“Financial institutions expanded and improved further in 2005,enhancing the capacity and capability of the financial sector toeffectively support the financial needs of the economy. The numberof insurance and leasing institutions increased. Although the numberSof banks increased with the establishment of a government ownedspecialized bank, the SME Bank . . . . . ”–

That is the Lankaputhra Bank.

–“. . . . . . the number of banks at the end of the year remainedunchanged as a result of the merger . . . . . ”–

Exactly what you were recommending.

–“. . . . . . of a licensed specialised bank with a licensedcommercial bank. Total assets of financial institutions increased by14 per cent . . . . . ”–

during the year 2005. So, these are the macro-elementsunder which some of your individual comments we maytake into consideration. But, certainly, we cannot take upthe position that we have been remiss in managing thefinancial sector.

Let me give you some other information from the CentralBank Report. Page 126, Chapter 8.2 further states underthe heading, “The Central Bank of Sri Lanka,” I quote :

“The country experienced signifficant amounts of foreign inflowsof funds through grants, loans, debt relief and remittances, whichled to an overall improvement in the NFA and resulted in a BOPsurplus.”

after a long time.

We are hoping to have a seven to eight per cent growth inGDP this year in a sustainable basis. So, the situation is notas you have mentioned in respect of various details whichwe can discuss. ªÇæ Ç ¨Çæ¸å¾åÆ¨ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ ¼ ¯ÝµÇ·Ç»¯Æ¯)(The Hon. Ravi Karunanayake)

ªÇæ ¡å®åÚÆ ÌÇºà ¡Äê¸êªÄ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ (¯¾Ç·È¶È) ±¼¶Æ £ÛÙ¯º)(The Hon. (Dr.) Sarath Amunugama)

What I am trying to say is that, today, if you take all themajor countries such as China, India, Malaysia and theEuropean Union countries the lending pattern is changing.Though it is government to government, the money is beingchannelled through EXIM banks. The basic negotiationsare with the EXIM banks. The governments are subsidizingthe interest rates which are offered to us by the EXIM banks.That is a new trend that is taking place in the world of bilateralassistance.

ªÇæ Ç ¨Çæ¸å¾åÆ¨ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ ¼ ¯ÝµÇ·Ç»¯Æ¯)(The Hon. Ravi Karunanayake)

Hon. Minister, that is why I said that the United NationalParty under the leadership of Hon. Ranil Wickremasinghemanaged to get US Dollars 4500 billion into this country.That was at a .01and .2 per cent interest rate. Now you arepaying 6 per cent, 7 per cent. (Interruption)

ªÇæ ¡å®åÚÆ ÌÇºà ¡Äê¸êªÄ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ (¯¾Ç·È¶È) ±¼¶Æ £ÛÙ¯º)(The Hon. (Dr.) Sarath Amunugama)

That is completely wrong. we are getting preferential rates.That is what I am trying to explain. There is an informationgap in this. Take the Government of China for example.They are not lending money on a government to governmentbasis. Our negotiations will be with the Export Import Bankof China. But the Government of China will be subsidizingor giving the differential to that banks, so that the bank willlend us at very low rates which we used to get under G to Garrangements. This is a system of lending which is changing

all over the world because most of the governments do notwant to have local agencies here. They do not want to haveunnecessary extra funding. They prefer to do it on a financialbasis, but the advantage to the recipient country will comewhen the differential regarding the concessional credit andthe normal EXIM bank credit will be paid to the bank by therelevant government. So, please do not worry that there isgoing to be a slippage in the growth process and a slippagein the amount of money coming into this country. But nowI come to some areas where I agree with you.

ªÇæ Ç ¨Çæ¸å¾åÆ¨ ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ ¼ ¯ÝµÇ·Ç»¯Æ¯)(The Hon. Ravi Karunanayake)

You would know, theNorochcholai coal power plant which is US Dollars 420million where Allan Watson the Switzerland consultant hadasked, we basically got for European standards, US Dollars420 million and the Chinese have come with US Dollars420 million. The same Chinese cost is only US Dollars 301million. So, US Dollars 120 million is for what purpose?That is the question. That is the credibility gap.

Hon. Member. Now listen to me when I agree with you. Weare facing a very very difficult economic situation. This isnot only faced by us. I was in Pakistan last week. They arefacing a terrible crisis. All their development plans are injeopardy. You know, India is going through not only aneconomic poblem but also a political problem because of theescalating price of oil. All the developing countries have hadto look at the growth rates, the sustainability of their projectsbecause of the rising oil prices and the highly volatile Middle-Eastern situation. Even as we are speaking now, you willagree, the Hon. Members of the Opposition will agree, oilprices are escalating. One oil specialist whom I met inPakistan told me, with the Iraq situation and with all theseproblems, he thought we could contain the oil prices at USDollars 70 a barrel. You include Iraq. You factor in Iraq to thisproblem, you will go up to US Dollars 70. But if you bringIran and if you have a highly volatile Middle-Easternsituation as we are facing today with the attack on Lebanon,there is a very serious and actual possibility of the price of abarrel going up to US Dollars 100. Now, that is a nightmarenot for the Government or the Opposition, not for the Hon.Ravi Karunanayake or for me, not only for Sri Lanka, it is ahuge problem for all the non-oil producing countries. So, letus not make a mistake.

This Government is going through an extremely difficulttime when it comes to managing the economy. And I want towarn the country that with this escalating oil prices, wecannot Hon. Bimal Rathnayaka, my Good Friend, we cannotgo on old economic surmises, economic theories because theworld and the global economy today is totally spinning outof control and our traditional responses, our traditionalanalyses are simply not adequate to face this problem. Iassure you very seriously as a Friend. I was one of the peoplein our party who strongly urged, the Hon. Anura Dissanayakawill remember, that we should have this Alliance and I stillsay that we should have this Alliance because I must say andI pay it as a tribute to the JVP that unless the JVP is animportant factor in this country there will be terriblecorruption in this country. They are the only people who arewilling to stand up and say, “This is corrupt. Please stopthis.” I have termendous respect for that. I think, you alsomust have respect. We can have differences of opinion abouttheories, about how we are going to take this countryforward. But I think, all parties in this country mustrecognize that they have always stood up, againstcorruption, sometimes it may be wrong, the information maybe wrong. You can explain and say that it is not so. That is adifferent story. But we need some people to stand up and say,“This is wrong, this is corrupt, this is not in terms of equity inthis country.” I want to pay that tribute to the JVP. I have haddifferences of opinion. We have argued with them. That is adifferent matter. So, we are facing a very very difficultsituation. And I think, this is not a time, I must say quitehonestly, that this country can afford to have industrialdisputes. We cannot in this global situation be carrying onour old little warfare, our old little battles. It is not a questionof what the UNP is doing, what the JVP is doing or what the

PA is doing. We are killing ourselves. We cannot afford thissuicidal, internecine rivalry when all the countries in theworld are facing an economic disaster if they are not oilproducing countries. Every day our budgetary projections,our budgetary plans have to be revised.

That is one thing. Sir, the second thing I want to impress onthis House is–in the global economy today to which we areinvariably connected whether we like it or not – Sri Lankacannot be out of the global economy. I mean, that is absurd tothink that we can be on our own without linkages to theglobal economy when our exports and a large amount of ourimports are totally interrelated to the global economy. Wecannot depend entirely on our agricultural sector as you havebeen advocating and as we have been debating. Every yearthe number of people taking to agriculture are getting less.Young people do not want to engage in agriculture. Ourmanufacturing and service sectors are expanding in oureconomy. That is the reality that we have to accept.

Of course, I agree that we have to improve our agriculturalproductivity. But now take the question of sugar. This issomething in which we have had an internal criticism and aninternal debate. Why are we not allowing the local sugarindustry to grow? Why are we bringing various tariffs and soon inhibiting the local sugar industry? I am glad that theHon. (prof.) Tissa Vitharana is here. His uncle, Dr. N.M. Perera,was the one who brought these reforms in excise laws toallow our people to tap Kithul tree to manufacture jaggeryand to manufacture various types of domestic sugarindustries. What is wrong with that? I am glad that I was ableto convince the Cabinet of that. And we are going ahead. Weare trying to develop that; stimulate that. So we have to lookat the total picture. [Interruption.] I think so; let us face reality.Hon. Lakshman Kiriella, you and I know that in our part ofthe country a large number of people are engaged in toddytapping Today the police go and harass them. The localbureaucracy goes and harass them. Sometimes they harassthat person and drink the very toddy and come off. Sometimeseven our own party members go and harass them and yourparty members too. So this nonsense must stop.

What I want to impress on, Hon. Lakshman Kiriella, isthat in the monetary world we have to constantly go onmonitoring checking and revising the laws. The old ideathat you can bring laws once and for all and close the bookis not valid today. If you look at all the bills that we arepresenting today, some of them may have little flaws, theremay be imprefections. But today managing financialinstitutions is an on going process. If I give a simple example,you may buy a car - now all have bought cars in this House.So as you go along there may be difficulties. So you have torevise it. Then you improve on it. So all the time it is thefunction of the monetary authorities - the Central Bank, theMinistry of Finance and this House - to be constantlymonitoring this and making changes. So as you go on makingchanges some Members like the Hon. Ravi Karunanayakemight say, “This is not enough. This is not answering the

(12.38 p. m.)

ªÇæ ï¨à. ¦¾à. ï®å¨àÌè ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ Í¯° ©° Ì±Ç¯Æ)(The Hon. K. N. Choksy)Mr. Deputy Chairman of Committees, I wish to address theHouse particularly on the bill entitled “Non-Performing Assets(recovery) Bill” the objective of which is to set up a NationalAsset Management Authority to take over the non-performingassets of the State Banks.

In my view, this piece of legislation is important becausethere is a duty to protect the financial viability and theliquidity of our State-owned banks. But at the same timethere is a necessity to protect the interest of the State revenuealso because in terms of the Bill before the House, the questiondoes arise as to the repercussions on governmental financesof setting up such an Authority to be financed particularly bythe State. The Bill provides for the Authority to purchase thenon-performing assets of a State Bank at a sum up to 90 percent of the net book value of such asset.

It is known Mr. Deputy Chairman, that there has been anaccumulation of such non- performing assets at the StateBanks, and the new Authority will have to expend large sumsof capital to purchase such non-performing assets. Whetheror not the Authority will be able to manage or dispose of suchassets profitably, or even to break-even, is an open anduncertain question. What happens if the Authority is unableto do so? That is an important question that has to be answered.The Authority starts - off with no capital of its own and so

clause 5 of the bill provides for the Government to makeavailable to the Authority such amounts specified by theMinister of Finance and voted from time to time by this House.This means a further loading of the already heavily over-burdened finances of the Treasury to enable the purchase ofsuch assets by the Authority which is in effect the State.

The mid-year Fiscal Report tabled by the Government inParliament at the beginning of this month and other officialpublications reval the following factors of concern :

During the first four months of 2006, the overall deficitincreased to Rs. 88.8 billion. Although in the same periodthere was a 19 per cent growth in revenue, the Governmentexpenditure grew by 24 per cent. The growth in revenue fellshort by approximately Rupees Six Billion to meet cash flowrequirements. Uncollected taxes in default stood at Rs. 92billion. The Report discloses a sharp 31 per cent increase inthe budget deficit during the first four months of 2006. If thisratio is to continue through the year, the total deficit for 2006will be 11.4 per cent, the highest on record.

The pressure on State finances is thus already sufficientlyloaded.

The Bill accordingly will overall be counter-productivebecause whilst assisting the State owned banks to dispose ofwithout complete loss their bad loans it will have a seriousnegative impact on the national economy. A differentapproach is, therefore, called for. This is imperative.

When the United National Front took office in December2001, the Bank of Ceylon, and more particularly the People’sBank, were in a Similar position due to the previousgovernment’s mismanagement of the economy. The Ceylonelectricity Board and the Ceylon Petroleum Corporationamongst other corporations were each heavily indebted tothe People’s Bank, which had been run down to the ground.We were, therefore, confronted with finding a viable solutionboth in the interests of saving the People’s Bank, whilst atthe same time. protecting the interest of State Finances. Thecorrect solution Mr. Deputy Chairman of Committees, wouldbe the strengthening or rebuilding of the banks themselves.The Bank of Ceylon possesses the capacity by itself becauseof its vey large private sector business segment. The Pelople’sBank has always been the more affected of the two banks. Arestructuring of the People’s Bank is thus the essential andlogical solution’ This bank has from its inception provided auseful financial service to the country by being banker tomany public corporations and financing their statutoryfunctions. However, the two public corporations which Ireferred to earlier, have once again made serious inroads intothe People’s Bank’s liquidity by borrowing and leavingunpaid large sums, particularly for the import of oil. TheBank, however, has a large network of branches throughoutthe country which brings it considerable business throughmiddle-level businessmen. It also collects large deposits,particularly, at the close of the two paddy harvests. It will,

2006 °ìÈè 1825072508

therefore, be prudent to consider restructuring of the bank’sbusiness by separating its commercial activities from its non-performing assets sector.

This separation will assist the bank to regain its financialviability and put it back on its feet. The commercial activitiesof the bank will build up the bank’s liquidity and asset base.After a period of time when this reaches a sufficient level, thebank will be able to apply some part to cushion theaccumulated bad debts.

Certain safeguards must of necessity be observed in thebank’s future commercial activities. Each decision by thebank to extend credit must be based on its merits. No politicalconsiderations should creep into lending to individuals.Individual borrowings must be subjext to prescribed limits,with adequate security. Mr. Deputy Chairman, in this respectit is found that there are no limits prescribed for individualborrowings. That must now be prescribed more rationallyand end enforced strictly. Furthermore, whilst the banks havetaken security for loans given, that security has ultimatelyturned out to be of very poor value. The banks are todayequipped with sufficient legal authority to take action againstdefaulting creditors. But what happens is, when it comes torealizing the security given, it is found that the securitydoes not fetch anywhere near the outstanding amount fromthe debtors. It invariably consists of run-down machinery orold buildings which are unsaleable’

The financing of activities of State owned corporationand the opening of Letters of Credit to finance their importrequirements must also be subject to guidelines. No doubt,such corporations require to be enabled to carry on theiractivities in the public interest, but the Bank has also toproted itself. The People’s Bank Act empowers the Ministerof finance to give the Bank special derections in the carryingout of its activities. The Minister could make use of thisprovision to lay down gudelines that will govern the Bank’sdecisions in the above respects.

Sir, I find today that notice has been given of certainamendments to be moved during the Committee Stage ofthis Bill.

The amendment to Clause 33 is to empower the Ministerto issue general or special dipecial directions to the Stateowned Bankd, in respect of certain of their activities.

This provision could be expanded so as to enable theMinister to lay down guidelines which will govern theBank’s lending procedures and particularly the manner ofthe assessment and evaluation of the security that the Bankswill be taking, bearing in mind that the realization of thesesecurities may take place many years later when they will bemore run down. So there is this provision which the Ministercould usefully employ to protect the interests of the StateBanks.

I suggest Mr. Deputy Chairman, that the Governmentconsider what I have just said as a viable alternative whichwill eliminate the need for the Treasury to increase theGovernment’s already near unbearable overloading of itsfinances.

The need to avoid State intervention in the interest of thenational economy is under scored by several factors inaddition to what I have already stated earlier. Risinginternational oil costs have increased the country’s importbill by as much as 21.5 per cent in the last five months.Exports have however registered an increase of only 5.8 percent. The trade deficit is thus growing. The Government istherefore compelled to spend more than it receives from thecountry’s trade and tax revenue. The difference is met byborrowing.Borroeing is now made enef in Dollars. Theupshot si unfortunately that of an economy that is rapidly inreccession. The wealth of the nation is getting progressivelyreduced. The development of the infrastructure andinvestment in much needed public projects, such as,electricity, irrigation, transport and rural development,including the important training of youth in self-employment activities, finds little financial assistance.

No doubt the rise in international oil prices has contributedto the above scenario. Yes, petroleum prices started increasingfrom the year 2002 onwards the then united National RrontGovernment identified straightaway what the long-termrepercussions could be.

We therefore, put in place an automatic price adjustmentscheme Under this, we increased the cost to the consumer,thouth not in its entirety as and when prices reduced.orrespondingly, we gave the consumer the benefit whenprices reduced. In this way, we effectively. cushionald theburden on the Exchequer. President Kumaratunga summarilyscrapped this beneficial sheme, without replacing it withanother, causing great financial hrm to the country.

The Rupee stood at Rs. 98 to the US Dollar in December2001. With prudent financial planning, we reduced the sameto Rs. 94 in April 2004. Today, it is back to Rs. 103 to 104against the Dollar. in a country heavily dependant on imports,this does give rise to much cause concern.

The overal result is that Sri Lanka is receiving negativeratings and some are even trying to represent that the countryis a ‘‘failed economy.’’

Sir, what I just said is not said as a matter of politicalrelish; it cannot be so to right -thinking people. Rather, it sia matter that requires a combined national effort and will tojointly cooperate and combat.

I trust that the Government will pay adequate attention tothe very arduous situation in the economy of this country,caused both by the on-going war in the North and the Eastand also by lack of adequate planning in the economic sector,and take immediate steps to put the country back on a soundeconomic programme.

I thank you, Sir.

ªÇæ ïÇåàÍúº ïÂåàïªåÈàÈåªÄ ÄÍºå (ÉñÉÌåÆ ÌâÉÚ½¾ Íå¡åïÆåà°¾ ÀòÉÚ½¾ ¡Äåºñ)(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ Í¼ÇÄÈ¶ Í¹ÇÌ¯Ç¾Æ¾Ç¯º - Ì¶ÇÀÈ¾Æ Û»½Æ±È£Ý¶Æ¶È, Û¶´ÆÕ ¨¯ÆÐ¹ÆÚ £Îº±Æ±¼Æ)(The Hon. Rohitha Bogollagama – Minister of EnterpriseDevelopment and Investment Promotion)Sir, indeed it is and opportunity for me to concentrate ontwo important Bills that have been presented to the Housethis morning. On this I would like to confine my submissionson the Non-Performing Assets (Recovery) Bill and on theBanking (Amendmend) Bill.

Both these Bills amongst the other Bills that werepresented to the House today are directly relevant to the co-business of the financial institutions of our country.

Today we are talking sbout a scenario where ourGovernment has come int office and we have been thebeneficiaries of a legacy that has got accumulated both interms of liabilities and that of a debt-stricken economy ofSri Lanka.

On top of that, as corectly articulated by the Hon. K. N.Choksy, the soaring oil pri9ces associated with the fuel costis again another challenge that we are experiencing. But inspite of all these challenges, as we have discussed in theHouse on the previous occasion, we have seen to it that oureconomy has recorded the hoghest levels of performance inthe production base of our country in agriculture, industryand in services in the first quarter of this year surpassing allother coreesponding quarters of several previous years. An8.1 per cent growth rate has been achieved. Today we arebringing this legislation purely because we need correctfundamentals in terms of the State Banks’ role in the recoveryof certain loand that have been given. I am sure that the Hon.K. N. Choksy is well experienced as a lawyer as well andwhen he was the Finance Minister I was a part of thatGovernment and we have discussed on numerous occasionsthe steps necessary to establish a more specialized onnumerous occasions the steps necessary to establish morespecialized approach, backed by a proper legal system, totackle the non-performing assets of our banking system.When the Pleople’s Bank was ridden with liabilities of aroundRs. 16 billion and was not able to recover them could its ownlegal department and the recovery department ensure therecovery of these assets? The Bank of Ceylon was also in asimilar situation. I have observed as the Chairman of theCOPE during the last year, when we summoned the banksbefore us how their capacity to recover these sometimes isrestricted. Some were restricted partially due to severalintervening factors such as establishment issues, legal issues,court cases, arbitrations and political influence making themunable to recover to safeguard the liquidity positions of ourbanks and to serve the wider objectives of the country. In thiscontext it is unfortunate that the far-reaching benefits of thislegislation have not been adequately recognized by theOpposition benchers.

We are talking about the micro issues associated with themacro economic scenario of Sri Lanka as at this moment.Whilst we, as a Government is maintaining the propersafeguards that are needed to sustain the economy against allthe adverse effects that is generated, sometimes beyond ourcontrol, but still sustain it in terms of achieving our furtherstgoals; the Opposition is unable to recognize the pursuit thatwe are advocating today. We have brought this legislationnot just to allow the liquidity situation of the banks to bemanaged only by the banks themselves. They get stuck attimes when they try to pursue their own objectives. That iswhy we need specialized legislation. Many good suggestionscame from the Opposition, well and good. But here, we arebringing legislation to convert suggestions into meaningfulaction. The objectives of this Bill, if one bothers only to readcarefully, will cover the entire gamut of what the Oppositionhas been seeking. The long title of this Non-Performing Assets(Recovery) Bill states, I quote:

“An act to specify the objects and to confer special powers on, anauthority to be called the National Asset Management Authority inorder to make the Recovery on Non-Performing Assets of State Banksmore effective and to provide for matters connected therewith orincidental thereto:”

According to this Bill an Authority will be created. It willbe very carefully supervised and directed by the Central Bank,the main arm of the monetary management of this country.The supervision is needed for the type of direction that isrequired when you designate a State Bank coming under thepurview of this Bill, and once these assets are vested tosupervise the procedure adopted for the recovery of assets.We have seen in the past that even the Central Bank lackedthis type of institutional capacity that was needed to recoversome of their own assets adequately and properly.

I would like to refer is some of the relevant issues duringthe course of this Debate today in relation to other institutionthan the Central Bank itself. I may quote some examples toshow their incapability at times. Without the proper type ofmanagement and specialization built into an institution likewhat is proposed in this Bill that we are discussing today,they may get stuck as well. We have seen the recoveryperformance of the Central Bank in terms of the loans thatwere granted to the failed finance companies. During theUnited National Party regime - I would like to just outlinethis because it is no secret since these figures have beentabled in the House before. I will just mention some of thefacts in Annex I sent by the Central Bank and I will mentionthe names of some of the finance companies because it isrelevant, why we are bringing in an Asset ManagementAuthority in the light of the need for us to bring correctrecovery process associated with assets management. We havethe figures of the Castle Finance Company Limited in the1990 to 1991 period, that was during the time of the UNP andtoday the ourstanding amount is Rs. 101.8 million. That isthe total (principal plus interest) outstanding. The totaloutstanding of the Hideki Finance and Investment Limited isRs. 107.4 million as at today. Home Finance Limited has a

2006 °ìÈè 1825112512

total outstanding of Rs. 309.7 million. Then we come to theSevana Finance and Investment Company Limited. The totaloutstanding of it is nil. The total outstanding of the TravelBag Investment and Finance Limited is also nil.Then, thetotal outstanding of the United Motor Finance CompanyLimited is Rs. 6.6 million. The total outstanding of the UnionTrust and Investment Limited is Rs. 800.1 million. Then whenwe take Mercantile Credit Limited, the total outstanding isRs. 5071.3 million and House and Property Trades Limited itis Rs. 727.1 million. Likewise, Rs. 7,137 million is stilloutstanding is other loans that have been granted by theCentral Bank and these were periods from 1990 to 1995 andfrom 1989 to 1991 likewise. So, this is the history.

Sir, due to the lack of institutional capability our countryhas lost out of the Central Bank alone this amount of moneythe biggest authority for the proper financial managementand all matters connected with the monetary management ofthis country. Now, here is a need for a special legislation toovercome this situation.

Today, the performance of the Bank of Ceylon came intolight here. There were wrong submissions made about theliquidity position of our two State banks. I must complimentthe two State banks for their performance. In the year 2005,again under a PA administration, the Bank of Ceylon in thefirst quarter of this year, it we go by their published figures,the profit before tax Rs. 3,203,216 billion as at December31st, 2005 as against Rs. 2,558,700 billion in 2004. That isthe profitability of the Bank of Ceylon today very muchhaving made all the provision for the non-performing aspect.

Then, we have the People’s Bank. There again it isperforming well now. I have got the figures of the People’sBank. The profit before tax of the People’s Bank in 2004 wasRs. 2,463 million and in 2005 it had shot up to Rs. 4,035million. This is the profit So, this is the liquidity strength ofour banks because they were well managed by ourGovernment both this year and the last year.

Now we come into the other areas which have to beconcentrated, I cannot see any justifiable reason as to whywe are bringing legislation than in order to support thedepositors who have otherwise lost out totally – Thoughtsare of no relevance, unless converted to useful actions. Ourthinking to do something in the years 2002, 2003 and 2004has no relevance if we could not implement it having had theGovernment for over 25 to 30 months in this country. Nowhere is a Government within the first six months of coming tooffice has implemented many things. We have said things inour manifesto and we are converting them ot action bybringing about the proper legislative mechanism in order toreach our goal; the goal to fulfil our peoples’ aspirations asthey have placed their confidence in our Government. It is inthat context that we have brought the Banking (Amendment)Bill. There was a major issue that was confronting Hon.Lakshman Kiriella whether there is provision in this Bill topay off the depositors. Why not? If you carefully read theBill, you would see that there is provision in clause 2 (3c) (a)I quote :

“Notwithstanding anything to the contary in this Act or any otherwritten law, the liability of the acquiring bank to meet the claims anddemands of creditors and depositors on liabilities of the defaultingbank vested in it shall be in accordance with the terms and conditions ofa Scheme which shall be formulated by the Monetary Board uponreview of the business of the defaulting bank and only to the extent setout in such Scheme.”

So, here is a scheme that is going to be implemented. Nolaw is going to give details as to what the scheme would beand how it should be given. We have legally provided for thescheme to come into effect. That is the value of this piece oflegislation. In the absence of it, people will keep on canvassingevery other authority in the country without getting redress.We have recognized that clearly and we have brought thelegislation to suit that. We are also bringing today theRecovery of Loans by Banks (Special Provisions)(Amendment) Bill. What for?

There are certain loans as articulated today in this Housesuch as mega loans, macro loans, big loans medium loans,small loans, micro loans and the tiny loans. We are consideringa defaulter irrespective of how tiny the loans is, irrespectiveof what bearing it has on the institution from where moneyhas been borrowed and to what extent the repaymentcapabilities of the borrower had been assessed. All have beenbranded as defaulters, debtors and they end up losing whateverwherwithall they had merely because the same law applieswithout discrimination. Our President as the Minister ofFinance does not want that to happen. He has been beforethis House echoing over times the need to protect the smallman’s interest because a wider base of this country still lies inthe hands of the small man. We have strengthen his capacity.

When he loses his pursuits, his capacities, his ability towork and situations become difficult for him, then he is beingtreated as if he is a billion borrower. It should not be the case.The law must have its own purpose of judgement. In thatcontext we have to have loans categorized. As Hon. RaviKarunanayake rightly said, what is Rs. five million today? Ifa person has borrowed or his loans oversteps Rs. five million,it is a differnet situation. But still if a limit of Rs. five millionis considered as a threshold, then why not he is given sometype of assistance to get back into the mainstream withoutlosing his house, his children’s assets, what he could haveprovided for the children’s education and likewise.

It is in that context we have brought this thereshold in thelegislation so that there is a divide between ones who havetaken loans, who have exploited the resources, who have notput that to good use for the country’s development and thatof the ones who have been pursuing their goals but tragedieshave over taken and they have got into difficulties.

It is in that social context that the President has thought itis best as a Government to bring in the cutoff point where wecan exercise certain levels of leverage to the ones who havenot the voice, the ones who have not got the capacity to goand negotiate with the bank, the ones who do not have thecapacity to obtain the best of legal services, the ones who

ÀòÊà¾Æ ÄÌ¾ È¼ú¾à, ÌÃå ÌÄº Æ¸Ç ÁÈÕ¯Æ¯¹ÆÌ¹½ÆÞ ª½ÆÞ¯ÆÌ¯Ç¿Æ¿¹Æ¹´Æ´×°Question put, and agreed to.¼ÍÍºàÉæ¾ø ¡å¸à¶ê¨òÄ ÉñÉÌà»åÌâïÊåà½¾Æ ¨òèÆåºàÄ¨ ¨ø×Ä ÄÚÊ¾Æ¨ø×Ä Ì¿Íå É¾ ïÊàË ¨åÇ¨ ÌÃåÉ£¼±È»¾Îº¹Æ 17¤Á× ¶ÈÝ¶Æ¶·´Á¯ÆÎ¯¯Î¿¯Æ ¯Á¸È¹Æ¹¶½Æ¯Ç¸ Ì¶¼ÈÐáSELECT COMMITTEE TO LOOK INTO THEOPERATION OF THE SEVENTEENTH AMENDMENT

TO THE CONSTITUTION

ªÇæ ¾øÄÈà ÌèÖÀåÈ ¼ ÌèÈàÉå ÄÍºå(ºÇ®ÚºÈÐ ·Èº¾Æ ±È½È¹Ç¾ ¶ ±È¾ÆÁÇ)(The Hon. Nimal Siripala de Silva)Sir, on behalf of the Minister of Disaster Management andHuman Rights, I move,

“Recognizing that the Seventeenth Amendment to the Constitutionof the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka has been pivotalto strengthening democratic governance in the country by providingfor the establishment of Independent Commissions;

And recognizing that subsequent to its passage, political partiesand civil society groups have made suggestions with regard tofurther strengthening the Seventeenth Amendment to the Constitution;

This Parliament resolves that a Select Committee of Parliamentbe appointed to look into the operation of the SeventeenthAmendment to the Constitution and make recommendations toresolve its procedural shortcomings and further strengthen itsobjectives;

2. (a) That the Committee and its Chairman shall be nominatedby Hon. Speaker.

(b) That notwithstanding the provisions of Standing Order95, the Committee shall consist of not more than twentyone (21) Members.

ÀåÚÈèï¾àºëÉ25812582

3. That the Committee shall have the power to :

(a) fix its quorum ;

(b) summon any person to appear before it, to require anyperson to procure any document or record, to procureand receive all such evidence, written or oral, as theCommittee may think it necessary for the fullestconsideration of the matters referred to above ;

(c) obtain the services of Specialist and Experts in therelevant fields to assist the Committee ;

(d) make interim reports from time to time and to sitnotwithstanding any adjournment of Parliament.

4. The Committee on considering all such matters shouldsubmit its report to Parliament within a minimum periodpossible of the setting up of the Committee.”

Subscription : The annual subscription for Official Report of Hansard is Rs. 2,178. A single copy of Hansard is available forRs. 18.15. (Postage Rs. 2.50) Copies can be obtained by remitting in advance an annual subscription fee to theSUPERINTENDENT, GOVERNMENT PUBLICATION BUREAU, No. 32, Transworks House,Lotus Road, Colombo 1. The fee should reach him on or bvefore November 30 each year.Late application for subscriptions will not be accepted.
